THE STUDENT'S GUIDE
TO
SANSKRIT COMPOSITION
(Being a Treatise on Sanskrit Syntax)
By VAMAN SHIVARAM APTE

PR EFA CE.
A glance at the Table of Contents will show that this edition differs from the :first in many respects. The a.dditions and a.lterations th.at have been made both in matttW and arragement require a few words of explanation.
The main body of the work is divided intofour Parts. The first Part
gives the general scope of Byntax and lays down the principallaws _of Ooncord. The second Part dea.Is with Government and gives principa! rnles in the Karaka Prakarana. In the third Part a1•e consider~ the more important Gramma#cal Forms, the meaning and use of which
require explanation; such as, several kinds of Participles, the Infini· tive Mood, the ten Tenses and Moods. Particles, such as are most frequently usedin Sanskrit Liteŗature, are also treated and illustrated being. alphabetically arranged and distributed over eight' Lessons. Some peculiarities of the Parasmaipada and Atmanepa.da-certain roots taking the one or the other pa!ia ac~ording as they are used ina particular s&nse or are preceded by cerlain prepositions-which were given in an Appendix in the first edition, have here been incorporated into the body of the work, and treated in two additional Lessons. The fourth Part gives matter not given in the first edition-the Analysi8 and Synthesia of Sanskrit sentences. lhave tried to apply the system of English analysis to Sanskrit sentences, and in doing so I have illustra.ted the rnles of English Grammar by examples from SauskriC authors, making such additions and alterations as were necessitated by the pecnliarity of the Sanskrit idiom. To some this portion may perha.ps appear superfluous. But my experien9e is that a correct knowledge of the relations subsisting between the different parts of a

---

[2]
eanskrit sentence is highly useful to the student, not only in translating from Sanskrit into English, but also in translating from English into Sanskrit, ~asmuch as it clearly bringa to his notice the diiference in conatl'llction between the two languages, and in composing senten· ces.. The general rulei; of Analysis are much the same in allla.nguages• but their a.pplica.tion is not ea.sily understood. In Section II of th1a part some rules on the order of tJJords have been giveu, mostly drawn from an ex~UDination of the construction of Sanskrit sentences and comparison with La.tin idiom. The third Section takes np the Oompolfition of sentences, where the stndent has to frame some senten· ces so as to apply the· rules of ana.lysis given in the first Section. Several exercises ha.ve, with this view, been given in this Section. 1 am inclined to believe that these exercises, if ca.refully worked, will give the student considera.bl~ facility in writing a few sentences of originGl Sanskrit on a ginn subject. The student has a.lso been shown how to paraphrase Sanskt1t passages, and it is expected tha.t, with the help of Ana.lysis, he will be able to paraphrase in Sanskrit as he does in English. The fourth Section treats of Letter writing, in which are given with examples and exercises, some of the common forma of letters. On th{s subject I have derived considerable help from ·a nnmber of manuscripts, dealing with ~:-forma of writing-that were brought to rri.y notice by Dr. Bhlmdarkar, and kindly placed at my dispoaal for some months.
This edition differs also in the arrangement of matter. Each Lesson here consists of three parts: the first gives the mies with illustrations; the second and third gives sentences for exercise. Ohoice EI1Jļ1f'6slfions and Idioma which were, in the former edition, gi_ven after the rules, and the Sentences for Oorrection which were given last, have here been given after the Notes. The Idioma have been arranged in the alphabetical order of the important words in their English equivalents, .anda good many taken from standard authors ha'te been added so as to increase the former nnmber by over one.half. The sentenoBs for correction have been promiscnously

[ 3]
arranged, and they may be attempted after the rules have been fullJ mastered. There is one more material cha.nge in arrangement that will not fail to strike the reader. The Sa.nskrit sentences have been divided into two parts: those in large type for reading in class, and 'additiona.l sentences for exercise' which may be read at home as additional reading. 1 have been obliged to maka this division, not becauso 1 eonsidered the number of sentences \>ery large, as some of my critics did, but because the sentences, as they stood, were too many to be read by students in the ordinary course of class lessons. I my· self felt the difficulty, while teaching the book ; and I thought it proper to do that which 1 myself did, and which other teachers also, who did me the honor of teaching it to .~tlir pupils, must have done, namely to effect a division of the Sanskrit sentences. This has, moreover, cnabled me to add undcr the 'additional sentences' severa-1 passages from authors not previously drawn upon.
The lesson on, the Nominativo case in the first edition has been omited, as it was found to he superfl.uous, and that on Prononn~ being out of place in Concord, has been transferred to Part Ill. The Appendix on the formation of the feminine of nouns and 'OO.jectives has been dropped.
Other improvements made in this edition· are two Glossariei-Sanskrit English and EngÜsh Sanskrit-which give the difficult words occurrin~ in the excercises for translation, and an alphabetical Index of all the nouns, adjectives, roots, &c., which have given rise to syntactical or otlļer rules. The want of the two Glossaries, more especially of the first, was much ·felt by students. The most ordinary -words which the student must ha.ve come across in his elementary course of study, have not been included. The importance of the
Index need not be much dilated upon, since it facilitates reference to
a very rema.rka.ble degree and is now regarded as a.lmost a. sins qua ·11<m of such works. For this 1 mnst thank Professor Ma.:t Muller ·
who was kind enough to suggest, among other thlngs, this idea oi

6'iving an lDdex. The Notcs, given after Part IV, retain · much of tbeir former charact.e:r. They artl mostly intended to be explanatory. Individual words, being included in the Glossaries, bave nothere been
repeated.
There are the main additions and alterations made in this edition. Besides, the work has been most carefully revised thrqughout; indeed, it will be difficult to find a page which has not undergone careful revision. Several rules have been recast; and many more, drawn from a closer study of Sa.n~krit Grammar.and the works of classicalauthors bave been added to each Lesson. Throughout the ·book severa.l Sanskrit passages have been a.dded, either to the illnstra.tive sentences, or to _the sentences for exercise. The effect of this and the.like addi~iuns has beon to increase the matter by nearly one-half. Yet by a suitabl4]! arrangement of types, the volume of the work has not much increased a11d that it may be within the reach of all classes of students, the price has been reduce~ to Re 1 as. 8. The rapid sale of a h~rge editim~ in less than three years shows that the book, in some measu:Pe, .;upplied a felt want; and it is hoped that the student of Sanskrit wiU find this edition more u,seful and a. better guide to Sanskrit coiil:position than the first on acqount of the improvements effected in it.

Before concluding I must not omit to tender ;my most sincer& tha.nks to Dr. R. G. Bhanda.rka.~, who was kind enough to spare time

to go over the greater portion of the book with me, and to make

severalimportant suggestions which have been mostly acted upon;

and soondly to Mr. Lee Warner, Acting Director of Public lnstruc·

tion who, at the recommendation of I)r. Bhandarkar, was pleased to

sanction the work for use in High Scbools. My acknow.l~dgments

are also due to Dr. Morell, Professor Bain,- and Mr. McMordie, whose

works have been useful to me in writing Part IV; and lastly to Mr.

M. Sheshagiri Prabhu of the Madras Presidency who was the first to

suggest the addition of Ana.lysis ~d Synthesis of sentences.

Poona, 24th. D~, 1885.

PART J.
INTRODUCTORY.
'SYNTAX' in English deals with the mode of armnging words in sentences, and lays down rules fņr the propor and correct arrangement of words. In Sanskrit and othe'r languages tbat.are rieb in inflexions Synta;c hņ,s not this deii.nite scope. The grammatical in:fle:xion itself shows the relation of one word to auother, and no harm or inaccuracy occurs, if the student do not observe the usual order of words ina sentence. Ta.ke, for exarnple, the English sentence, ".Rama saw Govinda. ". H the order of the words 'Rarna' and 'Govinda' he changeu, there will be a very great difference in the meaning; it will, in fact,_lJe a different sentence altogether. Take, however, the Sanskrit sentence for the same: UiĪl .()fci..I(+N~J:Ct:(. Here, even if the order of the words be
changed, no difference occurs in the meaning; the sentences 00
~iiM"<(+N~l.t€(, ~ ~llilsq~J:C~, ~~~nft ll'Wf..~ &c., all mean the sama thing. The order or arrangement of words in Sanskrit sentences is not, therefore, a point of great importance except in some cases, bnt this does not mea.n that perlect arbitrariness is allowed, and there are certain cases in which it is necessary to arrange words ina particular manner. In Sa.nskrit <hammars, rules on CvncOTd and Order

GOVERNMENT.
LESSON III.
The Accusa.tlve Caee.
§ 26. We now come to Governm.ent, the second general prinoiple regulating the grammatioal union of words in sentences. 'Gove?·nment' is the power which a word has to regulate the case of a noun or pronoun. The Lessons in this Part are intended to explain and illustrate this power.
§ 21. 'Karaka' is the name given to the relation snbsisting
between a noun and a verb in o. sentence. Thus &111 relatio~ subsist· ing between words not connected with the verb will not be called a
Karaka. There are six Karakas in Sanskrit: qīftf, ri, ~or, ~'
~ and ~or. These relations belong to the first seven oo.ses
exoept the Genitive, whioh is not regardcd as a Karo.ka case. llil'lf is
principally the sense of the Instrumenta!, aud means 'agent'. The nominative in ~anskrit, as in other languages, is simply the naming case, tha.t whioh is ooncerned in ~ 'predication'. Aooording to Panini II. 3. 46 ( ~qf(IJfiltffflnyqf'tmuŗčftņl(T~ IN1n') the nominative is uaed to denote the crude fol'm or ba.Ae of 1\ word, gender, measure,
and number only; as, ~:, ~:, ~:, wPi', ~:-t\'·t, ~ Jftft:, ~:,
1'\, ~~ &o.
Note.-Severa.l indeclinable worcls govern nouna in one or an· other pf the Karaka oases, and suoh casea, are then oalled ·~~· i, e, caaes governed by indeclinables &o., as diRtinguished from ~«·
~; oa.aes governed by verbs; as, 'Rit '[ft!lm, ilitfi'MI, Q'~ &c.
Tbe Iatter predominate over the former, where both are poeaibl&
( ~~: ~fiN""~6'1q~ ).

§ 28. The person or thing, upon whom or which the ejfect of .an actļon takes place, is ciilled the qbject of that a0tion. An object is put in the Accusative case, except in the passive voice; as, Il d(+N~~~

LESSON IV.
Verba governing two Accuaatlvea.

§ 38. There are 8ome ·verbs in Sanskrit which take what ie

called an a_kathita object, in addition to their usual direct one. As its

uame indicates, it is that object which is not otherwise 'kathJta' 01•

mentioned by way of any of the other case relations, such as~. 81~"1\(Ui &c. and is, therefore, optional. If the noun capable of taking

this akathita object be not intended for any other case, it is put in the Accusative case with such verbs; as, ~ ~ ~: 'he milks the cow
(her milk);' ~~ ITf 'he confines the cow to the fold.' Here ~ and llnt are akathita or optional objects. If the speaker do not intend
to have this object, the words will be put in their natural caaeaļ as,
~: (ablative) qth ~. riJ (locativa) at'i!C\Utf\ ltf.
§ 39. The roots that are capable of governing two accusative6

are mentioned in-the following Karik<i:-

§ 40. The roots mentioned above and others havin~ the sama
sense, thus take two objects. One of them is principal· and the
other,·secondary. In the case of the- first twelve roots from {1 to
m, .ne-, ~ the nouns qtf:, 'T\J"lt, ~f.r, ~. &c. are principal objects, and
rei, ~. &c. are secondary objects; for they can, accord-
ing to the speaker's volition, be put in other cases. And in the case
of the last four roots, ~ is the principal object and U'fli', the second·
ary. Thus that which is necessarily put ī'n the .Accusative case in
order to complete the idea of the verb, is the princz'pal object, and, that whi~h may be put in the Accusative case, depending upon the speaker's will, is called the secondary object.

nominative case, the other object remaining the same as in the activ• construction: e. g.

A friend from the South draws my attention to a
work called "Nayaniyam "-an abridgment of Sri-Bhagavata Purana-in which the writer does not inva.riably observe the rule given abo.ve. My friend quotcs two or three instances in support, of his sta.tement. l, for my part, should consider such instances ina.ccurate and unidiomatic, if not positively incorrect, constructions, ra.ther than modify the rule, by relying on insufficient evidence.

LESSON XIII.
::Participles.
§ 141. All participles in Sanskrit, except the so-called inde-
clinable past pirticiples or absolutives, are to be regarded as adjectivel, agreei'ng with the nouns they qualify, in gender, :number, and case. They are called 'participles' from the supposed participah'on or sharing izt the functions of the verb, the adjective, and the noun. The principa! kinds of participl~s in Sanskrit are these : Present, Past

---

<!-- page 53 -->

THE STUDEl!fT'S GUIDE

Future, Perfect, Potential, Passive, and !ndeclinable Past Participles;

(for the rules of formation see Grammar ). These participles obey

the sa.me rules for governing cases that may have been laid down regarding the roots from which they are derived. Present, Futnre and

Perfect Participles are treated in this lesson.

Presnt Partioiples.

;flŖ ( K. 141 ) 'ancl going, he thought.'

w hich serves to express an idea expressed in Fnglish by a. wbole sen•

tence. Obs. ( a) The 8anskrit participle must never be confounded,

LESSON XIX.
Imperfect, Perfect, and Aorist. § a0'1. 'In English there is only one tense referring to palt time;
it is the past indefinite or Aorist' (Howard;s Note cm English verb P. 12); as, 'I wa.lked.' In Sanskrit there are three tenses referring to & past time: Imperfect, Perfect and Aorist. Each of these had originally a peculiar signification. In ancient works, or works composed at & time w.hen, we have reason to believe, Sanskrit was a spoken la.ngu~ge,. they are found to he used in their exact senses; later on, as Sanskrif; beca.me less and less a spoken language, writers bega.n to use these three tenses promiscuously. The senses in wh~ch they were originally used are as follows:-
The Imperfect is, according to Panini, ~ ~•. i. e., it denotes past action done previous to the current day, hence at a time removed from this day. The Perfect is qUi ~ i. e., it denotes past action done previous to this day, ana which was not witnessed by the speaker. The Aorist is merely ~ ~.• i. e., it has reference to a past time 'indefinitely or genera.lly, without reference to any partic~lar time. An action done before to-day is expressed by the Perfect or Imperfect; what remains for the Aorist is, therefore, to exprf'ISS a past action done

---

<!-- page 74 -->

LESSON XX.
The two Futures and the Conditional.
§ 212. In English futurity is expressed by will or shall; in Sanskrit there are two different tenses to denote a future action; the the First or Periphrastic Future, and the Second .or Simple Future. The original distinction between the two is nearly the same·as between lmperfect and Aorist, except this, that the latter refer to a past time, the Iormer to a future one; in other words, the First Future denotes an action which is not to take place in the course of the current day, while ·the Second Future refers to a future time generally or indefinitely, as also toa recent future action. Thus the First Fututre denotes

---

employed to denote indefinite fntare time, to-day's future time,

recent a~d future continnous time; as, ~ ~mm:: 1

It is far better to support one's self by begging from door t door than to be a.lways fawning upon the ha.ughty rich.
Either he is able to do it, or his two brothers, but not any bod else.
It is but Just that he gives you ·this warning to spend mone economica.lly, for the marriage of your daughter is every day drawin near.
When calamities befall a man, discrimination is the real wisdon: for those who act without discrimination have their miseriAR mu tiplied.
a The poet tba.t said 'one fault merges in collection of goo
qualities,' did not properly observe human nature; for, generall, poverty destroys ev"ln a group of good qualities.
Who indeed, except this magnanimous person, would risk his lif to save that of others?.
Be sure, 0 woman, that you will ere long be united with you: husba~d; is it not a fact that a river, ·the water of which is dried u in summer, is again united with its current in the rainy season.?
I worship all gods with the same devotion, whether they belon.1 to the Yavans or Brahmanas.
I shall even prefer ( use Cf{..;ŗ ) a dreary foreet haunted by tiger and wolves, to a life led in poverty amongst one's kinsmen.
Woe me that have survived all that I held dearest on this earth
Oh! I have found the_ rin.g t"bat I had !ost.
Oh! how delightfnl is the appearanoe of this manP It ia just tha llie author of. the RAmayana used divine apeech to describe hi :ļllanifold deeds.

LESSON XXIX.
PARA~MAIPADA AXD AT11AXEPADA.
( N. B.-ln this and the ne:xt Jesson ·the unspecified .references are to Siddh&nata Kaumudi; and Ek. means the eight canto of Bhatti
Kavya. )
§ 308. There are two Padas in Sanskrit: the .Atmanepada and
Parasmaipada. The .Atmanepada ( 'voice for one's self' ) denotes that the fruit of the action accrues to the agent ('!fī;tmf~ ~); as,~ •aoes for himself.' The Parasmaipada ( 'voice for another' ) denotea that the fruit of the action accrues to auother; ~ 'goes for another.' Tļlis distinction is SC!U·cely, if ever, ohserved in practice. It is the ~riginal mea:uing of the terms, but cannot be consistently followed in all cases. Sanskrit writers use hoth the Padas promiscuously; as,
m- '*~ilif<:tlofi '>{ltļUt-..0iiJI ( M. I. ) 'I wish now to l1ere the message';
~qfuit~~Tfir(S'.4);~Ŗŗ~~ (.R. V.15.). If it be supposed that this distinction is meant to be observed
when a root admits of both tbe Padas, this is also not borne out by
:nsage; as, ~~ ~;1{11-;ft~~ ~ 'll4' ~ mr-m:itM- f~~~ ~~­
~ tftr wm:r ~ ( K. 74 ), where the two Padas are used in the same
..senso.
§ 309. Som_e roots are conjugated in one Pada only, as~·~.
n, \ll~ ~·c; some m both the Padas, as"' f'\:f, ~\.. ~{ &c., while somo
are restricted to one Pada or the other, according as certain preposi· t'io:ns are prefixed to them, or they are used in particular senses; as, ~is Parasmaipada; but~ is .Atmanepada; ~'to rule' is Para8maipada, but~ 'to bless' is .Atm. Some roots of this n~ttuze arA _given in. this and the next lesson.

PART IV.
ANALYSIS AND SYNTHESIS OF SENTENCES.
§ 346. lrt the first three Parts we have dealt with some of the
chief principles .that regulate the joining together of words in sentences. We have also explained the meanings and nses of the more important grammatical forms and useful connective particles, which, as observed by Prof. BaiD, 'belonging alike to all subjects and all styles, are the very hinges of composition.' The expla.nation of such forms and words is the more necessa.ry in the case of Sa.nskrit, as in the existing Sanskrit Grammars that subject is rarely oŗ very im• perfectly .treated, though, perhaps, in doing so a writer may seem to trench a little on the province of the lexicographer.
To render the rules of Syntax more simple and intelligible it is
necessary to consider the analysis of sentences. This will
enable the stndent to ascertain the different parts of sentences and the relation in whiqb they stand to one another. The analysis of senfr>nces will also facilitate Sanskrit composition, and help the student in translating from Sanskrit into English and vice versa.

§ 354. Tbe most general and frequent means Qf enlargement

LESSON II:
On the Order ofWords in Sentences~
§ 399. It has already been observed in the introductory seotion of Part I, that the order of words in a. Sauskrit sentence is no• &

---

<!-- page 132 -->

252
mc.terial ~int for consideration. In Sanskrit every word ( except. adverbs and particles ) is inflect.ed, and the gramm&.tica.l infiexion itiaelf shows the rela.tion in which one word sta.nds to another. Thus grammat~cally speaking, .there is no order as such that need be much attended to. .A sentence like ~ mllPf <{~ ~mi ~ŗroŗ: ~ may look very awkward bnt it is not gra.mma.tically ine01rect. But if there is no grammatical order, there is a sort of logicai sequence of ideas, whioh must follow one anotber in a paticular order. If we examine the pages of any Sanskrit prose work, we shall find that there is some order in the a.rrangement of words; e. g. first comes the subject e:xpressed or understood, with its adjuncts, then the object, ( if any ), and la.stly, the verb or predicate;
Ŗŗ ~;ŗŗŗ~ro~ S\f4+iqej(if;(r<{(ft ( K. 307 ); +iifi4Rt~\'i ~'lf{ ~ii4§+iiOČ ~~ ( D. K. I. 25 ).
Even in Kavyas and Dramatic poetry, which are 11oķnowledged to be exempt frol!l the rules of ordinary prose, this order is many times strictly followed;
~~ W:t ( R. I. 8 ); ~ ftm: q-A ~ ill' tNT: ( Bh. II. 77 ); <{4;0f"ff+i6<tl tmi'l: ~
(U.4); S{~lJt*Ŗqft~~ (Mal. 5) &c.
We shall now lay down some principles regulating the order of words in sentences.
§ 400. The best rule for the student to follow in arrnnging
words ina prose sentence is this:-First place the subject with all its adjectives and adjectival :phra.se~, then the object with its adjuncts, and la.stly the predicate ( verba!, nominal, or adjectiva.l ). Adverbs and a.dverbial phra.ses may occupy any pla.ce except the last; while oonjunctions ( except a few ) stand first before the subject. Thus the student would give a very aw kwļl.rd sentence if he were to say.
nt~ gtr: am"tlf ~m ~ ~ smPrritN' instea.d of saying 1(N ~ ~ RY.~tlltNf..~U gi\': ~ ~· ( R . .V. 35 ).

253

fo; § 401. When a verse is construed and put in ik proee order
~he above sequence will be found to he generally observed. Take,
mstance, the following verse:

The general rule may be now split into particular cases and we shall show what the positions· of the several parts of speech should be relatively to one a.nother.

§ 402. The first principle to be learnt from the general rule
is that words must be so arranged that the ideas will follow one another in their na.tural order, and the words in their• natural oonnection, observing the laws of the dependence of words upon one another; in other words, the governed and dependent words general11' sta.nd before the words on which they depend or by which they are governed.
Thus an adjective and the substantive qualified by it, the tranaitive verb and it.s object, adverbs modifying verbs, prepositions and the words governed by them etc., shonld be kept as Lear as possible ina Sanskrit sentence.
§ 403. When a. sentence has a simple subject and a. verb, the
former .sta.nds first; ~~~ ( U. 6 ). .Adjectives precede the subject;

of the subject and object of verbs, and the genitive, which expresses the relation of one word to another-may he regarded as 'adverbs'
for all practical purposes. The following rules for the position of adverbs will thus a.pply to the case-infl.exions also, which are so many 'extensions of the predicate,' showing time, place, manner or ca.use
and effect.

LESSON IV.
Letter-Writing.
§ 42'1. L~tter-writing is not a. subject to which Sanskri1 writers seem to have devoted much attentiou. We find very few in· stances of letters iu the existing Sanskrit works, proba.bly because 0111 forefathers did not much resort to that system. Natura.lly, therefore, letter-writing in Sanskrit does not present thedifficulty whicbalett81

To BUBKBIT COllPOSITJO:W,

lbY

in English, with its varions forms-private, commercial, official &c.-
nsually presents. Letters written in Sa.nskrit are mostly of one type. ~-'hereare certain settled forms in which they shonld be begun. Theŗe 1s also a. varietv in these forms according to the position of the person
to whom they- &.l'e a.ddressed. · E"J.t beyond this difference there is n~thing to distingui~;h a pnrely private letter, ( say, from a father to h1s son ) from the official or demi-official letter sent by a minister to
his sove:eign, or by any person to another person officially. We
propose m this Section to give some of the common forms of letterwriting in Sanskrit, with examples,

§ 429. We now "all the attention of' the student to the follo1fļ

ing points: 1. Every letter begins with the word ~. 2. The place where the letter is written is s~ated tfiri!t, as in

E,ngÜsh, and is put in the ablativ·e case, being. construed with the main predicate. It is sometimes put last in the loca.tive case, as in letter II.
,. 3. The address ( 'my dear,' 'dear Mr.' etc. ) is not actually ex-

pressed but is represented by some word expressive of that relation; as, ang~ indicating a younger relative, tir.ŗ friendship etc.

4. The name of the writer, which is, in English, usually coupled
with a word expressive of the relationship between him and the person add~essed, is not usually written at the end, but at the beginning, being made the subject of the .firll~ introductory sentence. The

degree of relationship is expressed in thepredicate of the first sentence ('&no~~· 'pays his respects to', whiob indicates that the writer is
a· friend of the person o.ddressed ; 'fct:tttC<i•\i\ ' that they are .mere
acquaintauces; 'q{tGC(~J<q ~· that the writer is a near relation, a.

father, husband &c.)

• These adjectives are merely complimentary. It is, however, usnal to put in one or two as a gracefnl introduction. They may be omitted in a pnrely business letter.
t When letters pass betw·een persona .in the.. same town the place is usua.lly omitted, as also the da.te.

N. B.-In modern forms the writer's name is put 1\t the end (118 in specimen II), in the genitive case, going with some word lik~ f<t~:, ~ in the body of the letter. It sh,ould be noted that this. style is rrore formal, and should be used when the writer does not
know, or is not familiar with, the person addressed.

5. The commencement, or thej01·m proper, of the letter is in the third person, though other persons may·occur in the body of the letter itself.

6. The namo of the person addressed which, in English, is somatimes written at the end, near the left corner of the paper, and is written in full on the envelope, is, in Sanskrit; given in the introduc· tory sentence together with the place of his residence, being made the object or the subject ( as in letter II ) of the predicate or connected with it in'a.ny other way. This gives the superscription or address of a letter.
7. It is not usual in Sanskrit to specify the rlate of writing: but when required, it is generall.}' put in the locative case, being used as an adverbial adjunct of the predicate, or put last at the left-ha.nd corner of the Ietter; as, tļllliļŖoŗ~ ~~ft ~ ~ ~ 1
